			pageInfo.article = {"inc_headline":"10 Words People Who Lack Confidence Always Use","inc_homepage_headline":null,"inc_homepage_headline_ab_test":null,"inc_sharing_headline":null,"inc_twitter_headline":null,"inc_title":"10 Words People Who Lack Confidence Always Use","inc_custom_byline":null,"inc_deck":"Want to avoid giving the impression you lack confidence and authority? Avoid these words.","inc_homepage_deck":null,"inc_sharing_deck":null,"inc_clean_text":"&lt;p&gt;Nine-hundred and seventy-two.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;That's the total number of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.inc.com\/christina-desmarais\/sanebox-email-doesnt-have-to-suck.html\"&gt;e-mails&lt;\/a&gt; I received just in May, and it's about my average. That's not counting the hundreds and hundreds of messages Gmail dumped into categories for promotional mail, forum posts, and social networking updates. I've become proficient at jumping through messages quickly (using the &lt;em&gt;J&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;K&lt;\/em&gt; keys), but there's one thing I've mastered even more than that: spotting a lack of confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I also take quite a few cold calls--people who are not really sure what I do and have not really done too much research but have me on a phone list for some reason.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;In most cases, it's a pitch about a product or someone asking a question about marketing to journalists. He or she might say he or she \"usually\" does something. In a few cases, it's someone with a business idea&nbsp;he or she \"suspects\" will be perfect. Most of&nbsp;the time, these messages are straightforward--the sender isn't&nbsp;messing around. But a few seem&nbsp;hesitant. I fire back a question, and the response makes me question the person's authority on the subject.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;These words are not always triggers about confidence level, but they are my&nbsp;first signal that something is&nbsp;amiss. They make me think the sender is not that sure about the&nbsp;product or service. And they are dead giveaways that I&nbsp;need to question what the person says.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;1.&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;Might&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Be careful when you tell people you \"might\" do something. Are you sure about that? No one is asking you to solve world peace. When you say you \"might\" finish a report, it implies you lack some ability, don't manage your time well, or have too many priorities.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;2. &lt;em&gt;Won't&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Here's an obvious word to avoid in your emails. Anyone who says he or she \"won't\" do something or \"won't\" attend a meeting is generating a negative vibe. Be more decisive: Either accept an invitation or reject it; using the word &lt;em&gt;won't&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;suggests hesitancy.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;3. &lt;em&gt;Usually&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;This is a trigger word in email that makes it obvious to everyone that you don't have all the facts. If you say the accounting department \"usually\" doesn't approve your expense report or the boss is \"usually\" late to work, it means you're stretching the truth.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;4. &lt;em&gt;Suspect&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Unless you are talking about a suspect in a trial, avoid saying you \"suspect\" anything. You're not Sherlock Holmes. Just use direct terms: You know an investor is pulling out of the project, and here's why; or you have facts to support your conclusion on a new marketing plan.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;5. &lt;em&gt;Impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I'll bet Mark Zuckerberg has never used the word &lt;em&gt;impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;in an email. The recipient will lose confidence in you quickly. State why something might be hard or difficult or just don't agree to a course of action. Don't bother telling people it's impossible.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;6. &lt;em&gt;Worried&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all worry about the stresses of life. Telling people you are worried by email makes it seem as if you lack confidence in your abilities. If you are worried, don't bother saying that to anyone--just express what you are concerned about and offer solutions.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;7. &lt;em&gt;Confused&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Expressing your confusion will create even more confusion. It's better to just say what you are confused about and ask questions. Saying you are \"confused\" gives people the impression that either you don't understand something or that the topic is confusing to you.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;8. &lt;em&gt;Need&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all have needs in life. When you express those needs by email over and over again, it makes you look needy. I \"need\" you to come to work early, I \"need\" you to get that report done. Avoid saying \"need\" and express requirements more directly.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;9. &lt;em&gt;Quandary&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Have you sent a message and said you were in a \"quandary\"? You should know that the word means you are in a total state of perplexity. I mean, you are &lt;em&gt;really &lt;\/em&gt;perplexed. That's not often the case when it comes to a new business proposal or fundraising round.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;10. &lt;em&gt;Likely&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Few of us are in the business of predicting the future. If you say something is \"likely\" in an email, you are expressing to the recipient that you are not really sure about the topic, and you don't have all the facts yet. It's &lt;em&gt;likely &lt;\/em&gt;that you just lack confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;","inc_pubdate":"2014-06-16 08:37:59","inc_promo_date":"2014-06-16 08:37:59","inc_custom_pubdate":null,"inc_show_feature_imageflag":true,"inc_image_caption_override":null,"inc_autid":null,"inc_typid":1,"inc_staid":7,"inc_serid":42,"inc_prtid":null,"inc_activeflag":true,"inc_copyeditedflag":false,"inc_filelocation":"john-brandon\/10-words-people-who-lack-confidence-always-use.html","inc_hide_commentsflag":false,"inc_hide_article_sidebarflag":false,"inc_custom_sidebar":null,"inc_show_read_moreflag":true,"inc_display_video_at_bottomflag":false,"inc_hide_video_prerollflag":false,"inc_full_width_read_moreflag":false,"id":58400,"channels":[{"id":280,"cnl_name":"Productivity","cnl_filelocation":"productivity","cnl_custom_color":null,"cnl_calculated_color":"FB433A","sortorder":0},{"id":415,"cnl_name":"People","cnl_filelocation":"people","cnl_custom_color":"FB433A","cnl_calculated_color":null,"sortorder":0}],"authors":[{"aut_name":"John Brandon","aut_last_name_for_sort":null,"aut_title":null,"aut_column_name":null,"aut_imgid":"6765","aut_blurb":"John Brandon is a contributing editor at &lt;em&gt;Inc.&lt;\/em&gt; magazine covering technology. rel=\"nofollow\"&gt;@jmbrandonbb&lt;\/a&gt;","authorimage":"http:\/\/www.inc.com\/uploaded_files\/image\/100x100\/john-brandon-author-bkt_6765_6765.jpg"}],"videos":[],"images":[{"id":37473,"sortorder":0}],"imagemodels":[{"id":37473,"img_foreignkey":null,"img_gettyflag":false,"img_reusableflag":false,"img_rightsflag":false,"img_usrid":0,"img_pan_crop":null,"img_tags":null,"img_reference_name":"_DO NOT USE_","img_caption":null,"img_custom_credit":null,"img_bucketref":null,"img_panoramicref":"shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth.jpg","img_tile_override_imageref":null,"img_skyscraperref":null,"img_gallery_imageref":null,"sizes":{"panoramic":{"original":"uploaded_files\/image\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth.jpg","1940x900":"uploaded_files\/image\/1940x900\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","1270x734":"uploaded_files\/image\/1270x734\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","0x734":"uploaded_files\/image\/0x734\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","1150x540":"uploaded_files\/image\/1150x540\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","970x450":"uploaded_files\/image\/970x450\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","640x290":"uploaded_files\/image\/640x290\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","635x367":"uploaded_files\/image\/635x367\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","0x367":"uploaded_files\/image\/0x367\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","575x270":"uploaded_files\/image\/575x270\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","385x240":"uploaded_files\/image\/385x240\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","336x336":"uploaded_files\/image\/336x336\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","300x520":"uploaded_files\/image\/300x520\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","300x200":"uploaded_files\/image\/300x200\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","284x160":"uploaded_files\/image\/284x160\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","155x90":"uploaded_files\/image\/155x90\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","100x100":"uploaded_files\/image\/100x100\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg","50x50":"uploaded_files\/image\/50x50\/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg"}}}],"slideshows":[{"id":93,"sortorder":0}],"formatted_text":"&lt;p&gt;Nine-hundred and seventy-two.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;That's the total number of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.inc.com\/christina-desmarais\/sanebox-email-doesnt-have-to-suck.html\"&gt;e-mails&lt;\/a&gt; I received just in May, and it's about my average. That's not counting the hundreds and hundreds of messages Gmail dumped into categories for promotional mail, forum posts, and social networking updates. I've become proficient at jumping through messages quickly (using the &lt;em&gt;J&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;K&lt;\/em&gt; keys), but there's one thing I've mastered even more than that: spotting a lack of confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I also take quite a few cold calls--people who are not really sure what I do and have not really done too much research but have me on a phone list for some reason.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;In most cases, it's a pitch about a product or someone asking a question about marketing to journalists. He or she might say he or she \"usually\" does something. In a few cases, it's someone with a business idea&nbsp;he or she \"suspects\" will be perfect. Most of&nbsp;the time, these messages are straightforward--the sender isn't&nbsp;messing around. But a few seem&nbsp;hesitant. I fire back a question, and the response makes me question the person's authority on the subject.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;These words are not always triggers about confidence level, but they are my&nbsp;first signal that something is&nbsp;amiss. They make me think the sender is not that sure about the&nbsp;product or service. And they are dead giveaways that I&nbsp;need to question what the person says.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;1.&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;Might&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Be careful when you tell people you \"might\" do something. Are you sure about that? No one is asking you to solve world peace. When you say you \"might\" finish a report, it implies you lack some ability, don't manage your time well, or have too many priorities.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;2. &lt;em&gt;Won't&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Here's an obvious word to avoid in your emails. Anyone who says he or she \"won't\" do something or \"won't\" attend a meeting is generating a negative vibe. Be more decisive: Either accept an invitation or reject it; using the word &lt;em&gt;won't&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;suggests hesitancy.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;3. &lt;em&gt;Usually&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;This is a trigger word in email that makes it obvious to everyone that you don't have all the facts. If you say the accounting department \"usually\" doesn't approve your expense report or the boss is \"usually\" late to work, it means you're stretching the truth.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;4. &lt;em&gt;Suspect&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Unless you are talking about a suspect in a trial, avoid saying you \"suspect\" anything. You're not Sherlock Holmes. Just use direct terms: You know an investor is pulling out of the project, and here's why; or you have facts to support your conclusion on a new marketing plan.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;5. &lt;em&gt;Impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I'll bet Mark Zuckerberg has never used the word &lt;em&gt;impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;in an email. The recipient will lose confidence in you quickly. State why something might be hard or difficult or just don't agree to a course of action. Don't bother telling people it's impossible.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;6. &lt;em&gt;Worried&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all worry about the stresses of life. Telling people you are worried by email makes it seem as if you lack confidence in your abilities. If you are worried, don't bother saying that to anyone--just express what you are concerned about and offer solutions.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;7. &lt;em&gt;Confused&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Expressing your confusion will create even more confusion. It's better to just say what you are confused about and ask questions. Saying you are \"confused\" gives people the impression that either you don't understand something or that the topic is confusing to you.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;8. &lt;em&gt;Need&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all have needs in life. When you express those needs by email over and over again, it makes you look needy. I \"need\" you to come to work early, I \"need\" you to get that report done. Avoid saying \"need\" and express requirements more directly.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;9. &lt;em&gt;Quandary&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Have you sent a message and said you were in a \"quandary\"? You should know that the word means you are in a total state of perplexity. I mean, you are &lt;em&gt;really &lt;\/em&gt;perplexed. That's not often the case when it comes to a new business proposal or fundraising round.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;10. &lt;em&gt;Likely&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Few of us are in the business of predicting the future. If you say something is \"likely\" in an email, you are expressing to the recipient that you are not really sure about the topic, and you don't have all the facts yet. It's &lt;em&gt;likely &lt;\/em&gt;that you just lack confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;","adinfo":{"c_type":"article","showlogo":true,"cms":"inc58400","video":"no","aut":["john-brandon"],"channelArray":{"topid":"415","topfilelocation":"people","primary":["lead","lead"],"primaryFilelocation":["lead","lead"],"primaryname":["Lead","Lead"],"sub":["productivity","people"],"subFilelocation":["productivity","people"],"subname":["Productivity","People"]},"adzone":"\/4160\/mv.inc\/series\/productivityplaybook\/productivityplaybook"},"commentcount":6};

and seventy-two.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;That's the total number of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.inc.com\/christina-desmarais\/sanebox-email-doesnt-have-to-suck.html\"&gt;e-mails&lt;\/a&gt; I received just in May, and it's about my average. That's not counting the hundreds and hundreds of messages Gmail dumped into categories for promotional mail, forum posts, and social networking updates. I've become proficient at jumping through messages quickly (using the &lt;em&gt;J&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;K&lt;\/em&gt; keys), but there's one thing I've mastered even more than that: spotting a lack of confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I also take quite a few cold calls--people who are not really sure what I do and have not really done too much research but have me on a phone list for some reason.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;In most cases, it's a pitch about a product or someone asking a question about marketing to journalists. He or she might say he or she \"usually\" does something. In a few cases, it's someone with a business idea&nbsp;he or she \"suspects\" will be perfect. Most of&nbsp;the time, these messages are straightforward--the sender isn't&nbsp;messing around. But a few seem&nbsp;hesitant. I fire back a question, and the response makes me question the person's authority on the subject.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;These words are not always triggers about confidence level, but they are my&nbsp;first signal that something is&nbsp;amiss. They make me think the sender is not that sure about the&nbsp;product or service. And they are dead giveaways that I&nbsp;need to question what the person says.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;1.&nbsp;&lt;em&gt;Might&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Be careful when you tell people you \"might\" do something. Are you sure about that? No one is asking you to solve world peace. When you say you \"might\" finish a report, it implies you lack some ability, don't manage your time well, or have too many priorities.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;2. &lt;em&gt;Won't&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Here's an obvious word to avoid in your emails. Anyone who says he or she \"won't\" do something or \"won't\" attend a meeting is generating a negative vibe. Be more decisive: Either accept an invitation or reject it; using the word &lt;em&gt;won't&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;suggests hesitancy.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;3. &lt;em&gt;Usually&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;This is a trigger word in email that makes it obvious to everyone that you don't have all the facts. If you say the accounting department \"usually\" doesn't approve your expense report or the boss is \"usually\" late to work, it means you're stretching the truth.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;4. &lt;em&gt;Suspect&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Unless you are talking about a suspect in a trial, avoid saying you \"suspect\" anything. You're not Sherlock Holmes. Just use direct terms: You know an investor is pulling out of the project, and here's why; or you have facts to support your conclusion on a new marketing plan.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;5. &lt;em&gt;Impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;I'll bet Mark Zuckerberg has never used the word &lt;em&gt;impossible&lt;\/em&gt;&nbsp;in an email. The recipient will lose confidence in you quickly. State why something might be hard or difficult or just don't agree to a course of action. Don't bother telling people it's impossible.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;6. &lt;em&gt;Worried&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all worry about the stresses of life. Telling people you are worried by email makes it seem as if you lack confidence in your abilities. If you are worried, don't bother saying that to anyone--just express what you are concerned about and offer solutions.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;7. &lt;em&gt;Confused&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Expressing your confusion will create even more confusion. It's better to just say what you are confused about and ask questions. Saying you are \"confused\" gives people the impression that either you don't understand something or that the topic is confusing to you.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;8. &lt;em&gt;Need&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;We all have needs in life. When you express those needs by email over and over again, it makes you look needy. I \"need\" you to come to work early, I \"need\" you to get that report done. Avoid saying \"need\" and express requirements more directly.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;9. &lt;em&gt;Quandary&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Have you sent a message and said you were in a \"quandary\"? You should know that the word means you are in a total state of perplexity. I mean, you are &lt;em&gt;really &lt;\/em&gt;perplexed. That's not often the case when it comes to a new business proposal or fundraising round.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;h2&gt;10. &lt;em&gt;Likely&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/h2&gt;\r\n&lt;p&gt;Few of us are in the business of predicting the future. If you say something is \"likely\" in an email, you are expressing to the recipient that you are not really sure about the topic, and you don't have all the facts yet. It's &lt;em&gt;likely &lt;\/em&gt;that you just lack confidence.&lt;\/p&gt;","adinfo":{"c_type":"article","showlogo":true,"cms":"inc58400","video":"no","aut":["john-brandon"],"channelArray":{"topid":"415","topfilelocation":"people","primary":["lead","lead"],"primaryFilelocation":["lead","lead"],"primaryname":["Lead","Lead"],"sub":["productivity","people"],"subFilelocation":["productivity","people"],"subname":["Productivity","People"]},"adzone":"\/4160\/mv.inc\/series\/productivityplaybook\/productivityplaybook"},"commentcount":6},{"inc_headline":"10   <script language="JavaScript" type="text/javascript">
   /*if( /Android|webOS|iPhone|iPad|iPod|BlackBerry|IEMobile|Opera Mini/i.test(navigator.userAgent) ) { mobile='TRUE'; } else { mobile='FALSE'; }
		welus = 'TRUE';welglobal = 'FALSE';weldesktop = 'TRUE';weltablet = 'TRUE';
		

		if(welus=='TRUE') {
			if(window.innerWidth&gt;800 &amp;&amp; ((mobile=='TRUE'&amp;&amp;weltablet=='TRUE')||(mobile=='FALSE'&amp;&amp;weldesktop=='TRUE'))) {
				// Special exclusion
				if (!(moment().year() == 2014 &amp;&amp; moment().month() == 11 &amp;&amp; (moment().date() == 4 || moment().date() == 19) &amp;&amp; adinfo.adzone.search('series/idealab') != -1)) {

					if (getParameterByName('cid').indexOf('ps')!=0 &amp;&amp; getParameterByName('cid').indexOf('pc')!=0 &amp;&amp; getParameterByName('cid').indexOf('ad')!=0) {
						overlay_ad();
					}
				}
			}
		}*/
		function getParameterByName(name) {
    		name = name.replace(/[\[]/, "\\[").replace(/[\]]/, "\\]");
    		var regex = new RegExp("[\\?&amp;]" + name + "=([^&amp;#]*)"),
        		results = regex.exec(location.search);
    		return results == null ? "" : decodeURIComponent(results[1].replace(/\+/g, " "));
		}

     <script>
      $(document).ready(function(){ });
					var stampChecker = setInterval("checkStamp()", 1000);
					function checkStamp() {
						if($(".leaderboard_abovenav &gt; div &gt; iframe") &amp;&amp; $(".leaderboard_abovenav &gt; div &gt; iframe").width()&gt;0) {
							if($(".leaderboard_abovenav &gt; div &gt; iframe").width()&lt;750) {
								$("#subscription_stamp").show();
							}
							clearInterval(window.stampChecker);
							if(window.stampTimeout) {
								clearTimeout(window.stampTimeout);
							}
							window.stampTimeout = setTimeout("checkStamp()", 1000);
						}
					}
     </script>

	$('#embedcontent').on('focus', function (e) {
	    $(this)
	    	.one('mouseup', function () {
	            $(this).select();
	            return false;
	        })
	        .select();
	});
})
           </script>
          </div>

            </a>
            <div class="narrowArticleHeaderContainer">
             <figure class="panographic-image panographic-image-a inc_editable_area ArticleMainFigure ">
              <div class="articleheaderimage picture inc_editable inc_editable_image" data-content-id="58400" data-content-type="article" data-editor-class="ImageEditor" data-fieldname="image" data-label="Main Image" data-parent-id="wrappercontainer">
               <span class="picture-inner">
                <span class="smallest small-between-medium" data-src="http://www.inc.com/uploaded_files/image/640x290/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg" data-src-x2="http://www.inc.com/uploaded_files/image/1280x580/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg">
                </span>
                <span class="medium large" data-src="http://www.inc.com/uploaded_files/image/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg" data-src-x2="http://www.inc.com/uploaded_files/image/shutterstock_184308473-covering-mouth_37473.jpg">
                </span>

               </span>
              </div>
              <figcaption class="pancaption imagecaption">
               <div class="pancaption_override inc_editable inc_inline_editable" data-content-id="58400" data-content-type="article" data-editor-class="InlineTextEditor" data-fieldname="inc_image_caption_override" data-label="Image Caption Override" data-variant="singleline" style="display: none;">

            </div>
            <div class="insideArticleAdContainer">
             <div class="insideArticleFlexiblePortrait" id="insideArticleFlexiblePortrait0">
              <script type="text/javascript">
               inc.adManager.displayAdSlot('scroll0','insideArticleFlexiblePortrait','insideArticleFlexiblePortrait0');
              </script>
             </div>
             <div class="padvertlabel">
              Advertisement
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="bodycopy inc_editable_area" data-area-class="Archetype">
             <div class="article-body inc_editable" data-content-id="58400" data-content-type="article" data-editable="true" data-editor-class="InlineTextEditor" data-fieldname="inc_clean_text" data-label="Body">
              <p>
               Nine-hundred and seventy-two.
              </p>
              <p>
               That's the total number of
               <a href="http://www.inc.com/christina-desmarais/sanebox-email-doesnt-have-to-suck.html">
                e-mails
               </a>
               I received just in May, and it's about my average. That's not counting the hundreds and hundreds of messages Gmail dumped into categories for promotional mail, forum posts, and social networking updates. I've become proficient at jumping through messages quickly (using the
               <em>
                J
               </em>
               and
               <em>
                K
               </em>
               keys), but there's one thing I've mastered even more than that: spotting a lack of confidence.
              </p>
              <p>
               I also take quite a few cold calls--people who are not really sure what I do and have not really done too much research but have me on a phone list for some reason.
              </p>
              <p>
               In most cases, it's a pitch about a product or someone asking a question about marketing to journalists. He or she might say he or she "usually" does something. In a few cases, it's someone with a business ideahe or she "suspects" will be perfect. Most ofthe time, these messages are straightforward--the sender isn'tmessing around. But a few seemhesitant. I fire back a question, and the response makes me question the person's authority on the subject.
              </p>
              <p>
               These words are not always triggers about confidence level, but they are myfirst signal that something isamiss. They make me think the sender is not that sure about theproduct or service. And they are dead giveaways that Ineed to question what the person says.
              </p>
              <h2>
               1.
               <em>
                Might
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Be careful when you tell people you "might" do something. Are you sure about that? No one is asking you to solve world peace. When you say you "might" finish a report, it implies you lack some ability, don't manage your time well, or have too many priorities.
              </p>
              <h2>
               2.
               <em>
                Won't
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Here's an obvious word to avoid in your emails. Anyone who says he or she "won't" do something or "won't" attend a meeting is generating a negative vibe. Be more decisive: Either accept an invitation or reject it; using the word
               <em>
                won't
               </em>
               suggests hesitancy.
              </p>
              <h2>
               3.
               <em>
                Usually
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               This is a trigger word in email that makes it obvious to everyone that you don't have all the facts. If you say the accounting department "usually" doesn't approve your expense report or the boss is "usually" late to work, it means you're stretching the truth.
              </p>
              <h2>
               4.
               <em>
                Suspect
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Unless you are talking about a suspect in a trial, avoid saying you "suspect" anything. You're not Sherlock Holmes. Just use direct terms: You know an investor is pulling out of the project, and here's why; or you have facts to support your conclusion on a new marketing plan.
              </p>
              <h2>
               5.
               <em>
                Impossible
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               I'll bet Mark Zuckerberg has never used the word
               <em>
                impossible
               </em>
               in an email. The recipient will lose confidence in you quickly. State why something might be hard or difficult or just don't agree to a course of action. Don't bother telling people it's impossible.
              </p>
              <h2>
               6.
               <em>
                Worried
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               We all worry about the stresses of life. Telling people you are worried by email makes it seem as if you lack confidence in your abilities. If you are worried, don't bother saying that to anyone--just express what you are concerned about and offer solutions.
              </p>
              <h2>
               7.
               <em>
                Confused
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Expressing your confusion will create even more confusion. It's better to just say what you are confused about and ask questions. Saying you are "confused" gives people the impression that either you don't understand something or that the topic is confusing to you.
              </p>
              <h2>
               8.
               <em>
                Need
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               We all have needs in life. When you express those needs by email over and over again, it makes you look needy. I "need" you to come to work early, I "need" you to get that report done. Avoid saying "need" and express requirements more directly.
              </p>
              <h2>
               9.
               <em>
                Quandary
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Have you sent a message and said you were in a "quandary"? You should know that the word means you are in a total state of perplexity. I mean, you are
               <em>
                really
               </em>
               perplexed. That's not often the case when it comes to a new business proposal or fundraising round.
              </p>
              <h2>
               10.
               <em>
                Likely
               </em>
              </h2>
              <p>
               Few of us are in the business of predicting the future. If you say something is "likely" in an email, you are expressing to the recipient that you are not really sure about the topic, and you don't have all the facts yet. It's
               <em>
                likely
               </em>
               that you just lack confidence.
              </p>
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="articledisclaimer">
             The opinions expressed here by Inc.com columnists are their own, not those of Inc.com.
            </div>
           </div>

})();

	s.linkTrackVars='eVar22,events';

	s.events='event3';

	$.post("http://www.inc.com/likeintercept", { incid: "58400", sharedtype: service} );

}

function trackEvent(eventtype,instance)
{
	if($(window).width()&gt;759) {
		platform = 'desktop';
	} else {
		platform = 'mobile';
	}
    $.ajax({
       type: "POST",
       url: "/rest/track.php",
       data: { incid: 58400, usrid: usrid, tempid: getCookie('incrnd'), abgrp: getCookie('incab'), eventtype: eventtype, instance: instance, platform: platform }
     });
}
